&lt;ul&gt;
&lt;li&gt;November&#160;7,&#160;2005&lt;span style="display:none"&gt;&#160;(&lt;span class="bday dtstart published updated"&gt;2005-11-07&lt;/span&gt;)&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/li&gt;

&lt;/tr&gt;
&lt;tr&gt;
&lt;th scope="row" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;"&gt;Country&lt;/th&gt;
&lt;td style="line-height:1.3em;"&gt;United States&lt;/td&gt;
&lt;/tr&gt;
&lt;tr&gt;
&lt;th scope="row" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;"&gt;Language&lt;/th&gt;
&lt;td style="line-height:1.3em;"&gt;English&lt;/td&gt;
&lt;/tr&gt;
&lt;/table&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;b&gt;Fuck&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/i&gt; is a 2005 American documentary film by director &lt;a href="/wiki/Steve_Anderson_(director)" title="Steve Anderson (director)"&gt;Steve Anderson&lt;/a&gt; about the word "&lt;a href="/wiki/Fuck" title="Fuck"&gt;fuck&lt;/a&gt;".&lt;sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-5"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;a&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt; The film argues that the word is an integral part of societal discussions about &lt;a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_speech" title="Freedom of speech"&gt;freedom of speech&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href="/wiki/Censorship" title="Censorship"&gt;censorship&lt;/a&gt;. It looks at the term from perspectives which include art, linguistics, society and comedy, and begins with a segment from the 1965 propaganda film &lt;i&gt;&lt;a href="/wiki/Perversion_for_Profit" title="Perversion for Profit"&gt;Perversion for Profit&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/i&gt;. Scholars and celebrities analyze perceptions of the word from differing perspectives. Journalist &lt;a href="/wiki/Sam_Donaldson" title="Sam Donaldson"&gt;Sam Donaldson&lt;/a&gt; talks about the versatility of the word, and comedian &lt;a href="/wiki/Billy_Connolly" title="Billy Connolly"&gt;Billy Connolly&lt;/a&gt; states it can be understood despite one's language or location. Musician &lt;a href="/wiki/Alanis_Morissette" title="Alanis Morissette"&gt;Alanis Morissette&lt;/a&gt; comments that the word contains power because of its taboo nature. The film features the last recorded interview of author &lt;a href="/wiki/Hunter_S._Thompson" title="Hunter S. Thompson"&gt;Hunter S. Thompson&lt;/a&gt; before his suicide. Scholars, including linguist &lt;a href="/wiki/Reinhold_Aman" title="Reinhold Aman"&gt;Reinhold Aman&lt;/a&gt;, journalism analyst &lt;a href="/wiki/David_Shaw_(writer)" title="David Shaw (writer)"&gt;David Shaw&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;i&gt;&lt;a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary"&gt;Oxford English Dictionary&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/i&gt; editor &lt;a href="/wiki/Jesse_Sheidlower" title="Jesse Sheidlower"&gt;Jesse Sheidlower&lt;/a&gt;, explain the history and evolution of the word. Language professor &lt;a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Nunberg" title="Geoffrey Nunberg"&gt;Geoffrey Nunberg&lt;/a&gt; observes that the word's treatment by society reflects changes in our culture during the 20th century.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Anderson was exposed to public conceptions surrounding the word "fuck" by comedian &lt;a href="/wiki/George_Carlin" title="George Carlin"&gt;George Carlin&lt;/a&gt;'s monologue "&lt;a href="/wiki/Seven_dirty_words" title="Seven dirty words"&gt;Seven Words You Can Never Say on Television&lt;/a&gt;". He named the film &lt;i&gt;Fuck&lt;/i&gt; despite anticipating problems with marketing. Animator &lt;a href="/wiki/Bill_Plympton" title="Bill Plympton"&gt;Bill Plympton&lt;/a&gt; provided sequences illustrating key concepts in the film. The documentary was first shown at the &lt;a href="/wiki/AFI_Film_Festival" title="AFI Film Festival" class="mw-redirect"&gt;AFI Film Festival&lt;/a&gt; on November 7, 2005, at &lt;a href="/wiki/ArcLight_Hollywood" title="ArcLight Hollywood"&gt;ArcLight Hollywood&lt;/a&gt; in &lt;a href="/wiki/Hollywood" title="Hollywood"&gt;Hollywood&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&lt;i&gt;Fuck'&lt;/i&gt;s reviews were mixed. Film critic &lt;a href="/wiki/A._O._Scott" title="A. O. Scott"&gt;A. O. Scott&lt;/a&gt; called the documentary a battle between advocates of morality and supporters of freedom of expression. &lt;i&gt;&lt;a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post"&gt;The Washington Post&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/i&gt; and the New York &lt;i&gt;&lt;a href="/wiki/Daily_News_(New_York)" title="Daily News (New York)"&gt;Daily News&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/i&gt; criticized its length and other reviewers disliked its repetitiveness  the word "fuck" is used 857&#160;times in the film. In his 2009 book &lt;i&gt;&lt;a href="/wiki/Fuck:_Word_Taboo_and_Protecting_Our_First_Amendment_Liberties" title="Fuck: Word Taboo and Protecting Our First Amendment Liberties"&gt;Fuck: Word Taboo and Protecting Our First Amendment Liberties&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/i&gt;, law professor &lt;a href="/wiki/Christopher_M._Fairman" title="Christopher M. Fairman"&gt;Christopher M. Fairman&lt;/a&gt; called the movie "the most important film using 'fuck'".&lt;sup id="cite_ref-fairman_6-0" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-fairman-6"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;5&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt; The &lt;a href="/wiki/American_Film_Institute" title="American Film Institute"&gt;American Film Institute&lt;/a&gt; said, "Ultimately, &lt;i&gt;Fuck&lt;/i&gt; is a movie about free speech&#160;... Freedom of expression must extend to words that offend. Love it or hate it, fuck is here to stay."&lt;sup id="cite_ref-vincelli_7-0" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-vincelli-7"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;6&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&lt;/p&gt;

&lt;li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-9"&gt;&lt;a href="#Release"&gt;&lt;span class="tocnumber"&gt;4.1&lt;/span&gt; &lt;span class="toctext"&gt;Release&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/li&gt;

&lt;li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"&gt;&lt;a href="#Impact"&gt;&lt;span class="tocnumber"&gt;4.4&lt;/span&gt; &lt;span class="toctext"&gt;Impact&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/li&gt;

&lt;li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16"&gt;&lt;a href="#Further_reading"&gt;&lt;span class="tocnumber"&gt;8&lt;/span&gt; &lt;span class="toctext"&gt;Further reading&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/li&gt;
&lt;li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"&gt;&lt;a href="#External_links"&gt;&lt;span class="tocnumber"&gt;9&lt;/span&gt; &lt;span class="toctext"&gt;External links&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/li&gt;

&lt;/div&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;h2&gt;&lt;span class="mw-headline" id="Content_summary"&gt;Content summary&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span class="mw-editsection"&gt;&lt;span class="mw-editsection-bracket"&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;&lt;a href="/w/index.php?title=Fuck_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Content summary"&gt;edit&lt;/a&gt;&lt;span class="mw-editsection-bracket"&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/h2&gt;
&lt;div class="thumb tleft"&gt;
&lt;div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"&gt;&lt;a href="/wiki/File:Perversion-for-Profit-putnam.jpg" class="image"&gt;&lt;img alt="A still shot from the 1965 film Perversion for Profit showing a man in a suit holding a placard. The placard features a cartoonish image of a leering man and the phrase &quot;It's a two billion dollar business&quot;" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Perversion-for-Profit-putnam.jpg/220px-Perversion-for-Profit-putnam.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Perversion-for-Profit-putnam.jpg/330px-Perversion-for-Profit-putnam.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Perversion-for-Profit-putnam.jpg/440px-Perversion-for-Profit-putnam.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;
&lt;div class="thumbcaption"&gt;
&lt;div class="magnify"&gt;&lt;a href="/wiki/File:Perversion-for-Profit-putnam.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;
The documentary begins with a segment from the 1965 propaganda film &lt;i&gt;Perversion for Profit&lt;/i&gt;.&lt;sup id="cite_ref-dvdverdict_8-0" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-dvdverdict-8"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;7&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;sup id="cite_ref-sampeczek_9-0" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-sampeczek-9"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;8&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;/div&gt;
&lt;/div&gt;
&lt;/div&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&lt;i&gt;Fuck&lt;/i&gt; begins with a segment from the 1965 propaganda film &lt;i&gt;&lt;a href="/wiki/Perversion_for_Profit" title="Perversion for Profit"&gt;Perversion for Profit&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/i&gt;,&lt;sup id="cite_ref-dvdverdict_8-1" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-dvdverdict-8"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;7&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;sup id="cite_ref-sampeczek_9-1" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-sampeczek-9"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;8&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt; followed by a clip from &lt;i&gt;&lt;a href="/wiki/SpongeBob_SquarePants" title="SpongeBob SquarePants"&gt;SpongeBob SquarePants&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/i&gt; which states that the word can be used as a "sentence enhancer".&lt;sup id="cite_ref-dvdverdict_8-2" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-dvdverdict-8"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;7&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt; The documentary includes commentary from film and television writers &lt;a href="/wiki/Kevin_Smith" title="Kevin Smith"&gt;Kevin Smith&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href="/wiki/Steven_Bochco" title="Steven Bochco"&gt;Steven Bochco&lt;/a&gt;; comedians &lt;a href="/wiki/Janeane_Garofalo" title="Janeane Garofalo"&gt;Janeane Garofalo&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a href="/wiki/Bill_Maher" title="Bill Maher"&gt;Bill Maher&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a href="/wiki/Drew_Carey" title="Drew Carey"&gt;Drew Carey&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href="/wiki/Billy_Connolly" title="Billy Connolly"&gt;Billy Connolly&lt;/a&gt;; musicians &lt;a href="/wiki/Chuck_D" title="Chuck D"&gt;Chuck D&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a href="/wiki/Alanis_Morissette" title="Alanis Morissette"&gt;Alanis Morissette&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href="/wiki/Ice_T" title="Ice T" class="mw-redirect"&gt;Ice T&lt;/a&gt;; political commentators &lt;a href="/wiki/Alan_Keyes" title="Alan Keyes"&gt;Alan Keyes&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href="/wiki/Pat_Boone" title="Pat Boone"&gt;Pat Boone&lt;/a&gt;; and journalists &lt;a href="/wiki/Michael_Medved" title="Michael Medved"&gt;Michael Medved&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href="/wiki/Judith_Martin" title="Judith Martin"&gt;Judith Martin&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;sup id="cite_ref-baumgarten_1-1" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-baumgarten-1"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;1&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;sup id="cite_ref-financialtimes_2-1" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-financialtimes-2"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;2&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;sup id="cite_ref-jackgarner_10-0" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-jackgarner-10"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;9&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt; The word "fuck" is used 857 times during the film.&lt;sup id="cite_ref-glenngarvin_11-0" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-glenngarvin-11"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;10&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;sup id="cite_ref-zoemak_12-0" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-zoemak-12"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;11&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;div class="thumb tright"&gt;
&lt;div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"&gt;&lt;a href="/wiki/File:Billy_Connolly_in_Fuck_film.jpg" class="image"&gt;&lt;img alt="Billy Connolly laughing" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Billy_Connolly_in_Fuck_film.jpg/220px-Billy_Connolly_in_Fuck_film.jpg" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Billy_Connolly_in_Fuck_film.jpg/330px-Billy_Connolly_in_Fuck_film.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Billy_Connolly_in_Fuck_film.jpg/440px-Billy_Connolly_in_Fuck_film.jpg 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="486" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;
&lt;div class="thumbcaption"&gt;
&lt;div class="magnify"&gt;&lt;a href="/wiki/File:Billy_Connolly_in_Fuck_film.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;
&lt;a href="/wiki/Billy_Connolly" title="Billy Connolly"&gt;Billy Connolly&lt;/a&gt; reflects in the film on the versatility of the word and its ability to communicate across languages.&lt;sup id="cite_ref-peterbradshaw_13-0" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-peterbradshaw-13"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;12&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;/div&gt;
&lt;/div&gt;
&lt;/div&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Scholarly analysis is provided by &lt;i&gt;&lt;a href="/wiki/Maledicta" title="Maledicta"&gt;Maledicta&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/i&gt; publisher &lt;a href="/wiki/Reinhold_Aman" title="Reinhold Aman"&gt;Reinhold Aman&lt;/a&gt;, journalism analyst &lt;a href="/wiki/David_Shaw_(writer)" title="David Shaw (writer)"&gt;David Shaw&lt;/a&gt;&lt;sup id="cite_ref-sampeczek_9-2" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-sampeczek-9"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;8&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;sup id="cite_ref-aoscott_14-0" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-aoscott-14"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;13&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt; and &lt;i&gt;&lt;a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary"&gt;Oxford English Dictionary&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/i&gt; editor &lt;a href="/wiki/Jesse_Sheidlower" title="Jesse Sheidlower"&gt;Jesse Sheidlower&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;sup id="cite_ref-stamets_15-0" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-stamets-15"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;14&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;sup id="cite_ref-charlesbritton_16-0" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-charlesbritton-16"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;15&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-17"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;16&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt; Language professor &lt;a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Nunberg" title="Geoffrey Nunberg"&gt;Geoffrey Nunberg&lt;/a&gt; says, "You could think of that [word] as standing in for most of the changes that happened in the 20th century, at least many of the important ones".&lt;sup id="cite_ref-stamets_15-1" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-stamets-15"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;14&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;The film next features author &lt;a href="/wiki/Hunter_S._Thompson" title="Hunter S. Thompson"&gt;Hunter S. Thompson&lt;/a&gt; in his final documented interview. &lt;i&gt;Fuck&lt;/i&gt; later includes archival footage of comedians &lt;a href="/wiki/Lenny_Bruce" title="Lenny Bruce"&gt;Lenny Bruce&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href="/wiki/George_Carlin" title="George Carlin"&gt;George Carlin&lt;/a&gt;, and analysis of the word's use in popular culture, from &lt;i&gt;&lt;a href="/wiki/MASH_(film)" title="MASH (film)"&gt;MASH&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/i&gt; (1970) to &lt;i&gt;&lt;a href="/wiki/Scarface_(1983_film)" title="Scarface (1983 film)"&gt;Scarface&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/i&gt; (1983) and &lt;i&gt;&lt;a href="/wiki/Clerks" title="Clerks"&gt;Clerks&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/i&gt; (1994).&lt;sup id="cite_ref-jackgarner_10-1" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-jackgarner-10"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;9&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt; Carlin's 1972 monologue "&lt;a href="/wiki/Seven_dirty_words" title="Seven dirty words"&gt;Seven Words You Can Never Say on Television&lt;/a&gt;" is excerpted in the film.&lt;sup id="cite_ref-jeffvice_18-0" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-jeffvice-18"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;17&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt; Journalist &lt;a href="/wiki/Sam_Donaldson" title="Sam Donaldson"&gt;Sam Donaldson&lt;/a&gt; comments on the versatility of "fuck": "It's one of those all-purpose words."&lt;sup id="cite_ref-sandiego_19-0" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-sandiego-19"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;18&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt; Bill Maher comments, "It's the ultimate bad word",&lt;sup id="cite_ref-dvdverdict_8-3" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-dvdverdict-8"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;7&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt; observing that thanks to Lenny Bruce, &lt;a href="/wiki/Comedy_club" title="Comedy club"&gt;comedy clubs&lt;/a&gt; have become "the freest free-speech zone" in the United States.&lt;sup id="cite_ref-aoscott_14-1" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-aoscott-14"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;13&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Connolly states that "fuck" "sounds exactly like what it is",&lt;sup id="cite_ref-sandiego_19-1" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-sandiego-19"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;18&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt; noting that the emotional impact of saying "fuck off" cannot be translated.&lt;sup id="cite_ref-dvdverdict_8-4" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-dvdverdict-8"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;7&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;sup id="cite_ref-sampeczek_9-3" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-sampeczek-9"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;8&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt; He says that if you are in &lt;a href="/wiki/Lhasa,_Tibet" title="Lhasa, Tibet" class="mw-redirect"&gt;Lhasa&lt;/a&gt; airport and someone is fiddling with your luggage, yelling "fuck off" will effectively communicate that they should stop and leave.&lt;sup id="cite_ref-peterbradshaw_13-1" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-peterbradshaw-13"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;12&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt; Morissette says, "The f-word is special. Everybody uses the word 'breakfast', but not everyone feels comfortable using the word 'fuck' so there's an extra power behind it."&lt;sup id="cite_ref-rowanwalker_20-0" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-rowanwalker-20"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;19&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt; Boone argues for less use of the word, saying that he uses his surname instead.&lt;sup id="cite_ref-davewhite_21-0" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-davewhite-21"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;20&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-22"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;21&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt; Radio talk show host &lt;a href="/wiki/Dennis_Prager" title="Dennis Prager"&gt;Dennis Prager&lt;/a&gt; says that it is acceptable for youths to hear the word on television and film, but not from their family members.&lt;sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-23"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;22&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt; In the film, opponents of the word "fuck" use an argument commonly known as "&lt;a href="/wiki/Think_of_the_children" title="Think of the children"&gt;Think of the children&lt;/a&gt;".&lt;sup id="cite_ref-noelmurray_24-0" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-noelmurray-24"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;23&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-26"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;b&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&lt;i&gt;Fuck&lt;/i&gt; observes that the original use of the word is unknown to scholars,&lt;sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-27"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;25&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt; noting that its earliest written appearance was in the 1475 poem "&lt;a href="/wiki/Flen_flyys" title="Flen flyys"&gt;Flen flyys&lt;/a&gt;".&lt;sup id="cite_ref-sampeczek_9-4" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-sampeczek-9"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;8&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;sup id="cite_ref-moiramacdonald_28-0" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-moiramacdonald-28"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;26&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt; It was not, as is often claimed, originally an &lt;a href="/wiki/Acronym" title="Acronym"&gt;acronym&lt;/a&gt; for "For Unlawful Carnal Knowledge" or "Fornication Under Consent of the King".&lt;sup id="cite_ref-dvdtalk_29-0" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-dvdtalk-29"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;27&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;sup id="cite_ref-bloodmoon_30-0" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-bloodmoon-30"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;28&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;sup id="cite_ref-micklasalle_31-0" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-micklasalle-31"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;29&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt; The word has been used by authors including &lt;a href="/wiki/Robert_Burns" title="Robert Burns"&gt;Robert Burns&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a href="/wiki/D._H._Lawrence" title="D. H. Lawrence"&gt;D. H. Lawrence&lt;/a&gt; (in his 1928 &lt;i&gt;&lt;a href="/wiki/Lady_Chatterley%27s_Lover" title="Lady Chatterley's Lover"&gt;Lady Chatterley's Lover&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/i&gt;) and &lt;a href="/wiki/James_Joyce" title="James Joyce"&gt;James Joyce&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;sup id="cite_ref-rowanwalker_20-1" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-rowanwalker-20"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;19&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;sup id="cite_ref-kenfox_32-0" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-kenfox-32"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;30&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt; The film explains that "fuck" established its current usage during the First and Second World Wars,&lt;sup id="cite_ref-wesleymorris_33-0" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-wesleymorris-33"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;31&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt; and was used by General &lt;a href="/wiki/George_S._Patton" title="George S. Patton"&gt;George S. Patton&lt;/a&gt; in a speech to his forces who were about to enter France.&lt;sup id="cite_ref-glenngarvin_11-1" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-glenngarvin-11"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;10&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;sup id="cite_ref-robnelson_34-0" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-robnelson-34"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;32&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&lt;i&gt;Fuck&lt;/i&gt; states that the first use of the word in a large-studio film was in &lt;i&gt;M*A*S*H&lt;/i&gt; (1970),&lt;sup id="cite_ref-moiramacdonald_28-1" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-moiramacdonald-28"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;26&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-35"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;33&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt; and it entered the &lt;i&gt;Oxford English Dictionary&lt;/i&gt; in 1972.&lt;sup id="cite_ref-sampeczek_9-5" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-sampeczek-9"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;8&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt; That year, the word was also recorded during the &lt;a href="/wiki/Apollo_16" title="Apollo 16"&gt;Apollo 16&lt;/a&gt; United States mission to &lt;a href="/wiki/Moon_landing" title="Moon landing"&gt;land on the Moon&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-36"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;34&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt; The film includes a segment from the 1987 film &lt;i&gt;&lt;a href="/wiki/Planes,_Trains_and_Automobiles" title="Planes, Trains and Automobiles"&gt;Planes, Trains and Automobiles&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/i&gt; with actor &lt;a href="/wiki/Steve_Martin" title="Steve Martin"&gt;Steve Martin&lt;/a&gt;, in which "fuck" is repeated for comedic effect.&lt;sup id="cite_ref-sampeczek_9-6" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-sampeczek-9"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;8&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt; &lt;i&gt;Fuck&lt;/i&gt; states that the most financially successful &lt;a href="/wiki/Live_action" title="Live action"&gt;live action&lt;/a&gt; comedy film to date had the suggestive title of &lt;i&gt;&lt;a href="/wiki/Meet_the_Fockers" title="Meet the Fockers"&gt;Meet the Fockers&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/i&gt; (2004).&lt;sup id="cite_ref-sampeczek_9-7" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-sampeczek-9"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;8&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt; The director analyzes the uses and connotations of "fuck" and the feelings it evokes on several levels.&lt;sup id="cite_ref-jackgarner_10-2" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-jackgarner-10"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;9&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt; Bruce is quoted as saying, "If you can't say 'fuck', you can't say 'fuck the government'".&lt;sup id="cite_ref-sandiego_19-2" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-sandiego-19"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;18&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt; Steve Anderson argues that "fuck" is an integral part of societal discussions about &lt;a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_speech" title="Freedom of speech"&gt;freedom of speech&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href="/wiki/Censorship" title="Censorship"&gt;censorship&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-37"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;35&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;sup id="cite_ref-keough_38-0" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-keough-38"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;36&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;sup id="cite_ref-sallyfoster_39-0" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-sallyfoster-39"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;37&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;h2&gt;&lt;span class="mw-headline" id="Soundtrack"&gt;Soundtrack&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span class="mw-editsection"&gt;&lt;span class="mw-editsection-bracket"&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;&lt;a href="/w/index.php?title=Fuck_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Soundtrack"&gt;edit&lt;/a&gt;&lt;span class="mw-editsection-bracket"&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/h2&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&lt;i&gt;Fuck&lt;/i&gt; includes songs with similarly themed titles, including "&lt;a href="/wiki/Shut_Up_and_Fuck" title="Shut Up and Fuck" class="mw-redirect"&gt;Shut Up and Fuck&lt;/a&gt;" by American &lt;a href="/wiki/Hard_rock" title="Hard rock"&gt;hard rock&lt;/a&gt; band &lt;a href="/wiki/Betty_Blowtorch" title="Betty Blowtorch"&gt;Betty Blowtorch&lt;/a&gt;, "Fucking Fucking Fuck" by Splatpattern and "&lt;a href="/wiki/I_Love_to_Say_Fuck" title="I Love to Say Fuck" class="mw-redirect"&gt;I Love to Say Fuck&lt;/a&gt;" by American &lt;a href="/wiki/Horror_punk" title="Horror punk"&gt;horror punk&lt;/a&gt; supergroup &lt;a href="/wiki/Murderdolls" title="Murderdolls"&gt;Murderdolls&lt;/a&gt;. Journalist Sam Peczek of &lt;i&gt;Culture Wars&lt;/i&gt; compared the film's music to that in &lt;a href="/wiki/Softcore_pornography" title="Softcore pornography"&gt;softcore pornography&lt;/a&gt;, and observed that the soundtrack was broad in scope and helped accentuate the film's content.&lt;sup id="cite_ref-sampeczek_9-8" class="reference"&gt;&lt;a href="#cite_note-sampeczek-9"&gt;&lt;span&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;8&lt;span&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;h3&gt;&lt;span class="mw-headline" id="Track_listing"&gt;Track listing&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span class="mw-editsection"&gt;&lt;span class="mw-editsection-bracket"&gt;[&lt;/span&gt;&lt;a href="/w/index.php?title=Fuck_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Track listing"&gt;edit&lt;/a&gt;&lt;span class="mw-editsection-bracket"&gt;]&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/h3&gt;
